The Catalogue of Unfinished Journeys

Chapter 1

CHAPTER 1

The first time I saw her, she was weeping over a dead pigeon. Not elegantly, not with a single silver tear tracking down a marble cheek. This was a catastrophic, snotty, shoulder-shaking event. She was crouched on the wet pavement of Crosby Street, cradling the iridescent grey body in her bare hands, her phone discarded beside her, screen cracked from the drop. A delivery guy on a throttle-shrieking e-bike swerved around her, yelling something lost to the wind. She didn’t flinch.

I was a ghost in the stream, paused. My own phone was a live thing in my palm, vibrating with three separate conversations, a fourth notification pulsing at the corner of my eye through my glasses. I was late. I was always late. But the raw, unmediated grief of it—for a bird, in the middle of a Tuesday—froze my scroll.

“Hey,” I said. The word was a dry leaf, useless.

She looked up. Her eyes were the colour of a storm over the Atlantic, that deep, bruised grey-green. They weren’t pretty. They were actual. They were wrecked.

“He was following me,” she said. Her voice was hoarse, scraped clean. “For six blocks. He’d land, look at me, hop, fly to the next awning. A co-conspirator. Then a window box fell. Or someone threw it. It was… definitive.”

I looked at the pigeon. Its neck was at a very final angle. A tiny bead of blood, like a misplaced pixel, gleamed by its beak. “I’m sorry.”

“Don’t be sorry,” she snapped, the grief sharpening into a point. “Be appalled. The violence of the incidental. The casual murder of a narrative.” She gently laid the bird in the recess of a closed utility door, arranging its wings as if for flight. She wiped her hands on her jeans, leaving smears of grey and a faint red. She stood. She was taller than I expected. “You’re in my light.”

I stepped aside. The sodium-vapor glow of a streetlight fell directly on the pigeon’s body, making it a small, tragic sculpture. She took a picture with her cracked phone. The shutter sound was an anachronism.

“Are you going to post that?” I asked, my own professional curiosity, a reflex, kicking in.

She looked at me as if I’d suggested she eat it. “It’s for the archive. The Catalogue of Unfinished Journeys.” She scooped up her bag—a worn leather satchel bleeding stuffing at one seam—and started walking north, not looking back.

I should have let her go. The ghost should re-enter the stream. My glasses pulsed again: a reminder for the 8:30 PM check-in with the Singapore team. But my feet were stupid. They followed.

“What catalogue?”

She didn’t slow. “Personal project. I document things that were en route but got… interrupted. Pigeons. Ice cream cones dropped upside down. One-sided phone conversations overheard and hung up on. The last biscuit in the packet, crushed. That sort of thing.”

“That’s… morbid.”

“It’s honest.” She cut left onto Howard, the wind funneling down the canyon of buildings and taking her hair, a chaotic mess of dark brown with a single, deliberate streak of electric blue, horizontal across her vision. “You’re following me now. Is this part of your narrative?”

“I don’t have a narrative,” I said, and it was the truest thing I’d said all week. “I have deliverables.”

She laughed, a short, surprised bark. “Deliverables. God. You’re one of those.”

“One of what?”

“The perpetually transmitting. I can see the glow on your face. The residual data.” She glanced at me. “You’re a ghost with a full inbox.”

We reached the entrance to her building, a narrow wedge of old brick between two glass towers. She didn’t fumble for keys; she placed her palm against a rusted metal plate beside the door. A heavy click echoed from within. Analog magic.

“This is me,” she said. She turned, blocking the doorway. The storm in her eyes had calmed to a watchful, choppy sea. “You have a name, ghost?”

“Leo.”

“Leo.” She tested it. “I’m Wren. Like the bird, but smaller and more prone to hiding in abandoned infrastructure.” She didn’t offer a hand. “Do you want to come up and see the archive?”

It wasn’t a question. It was a test. A binary gate. Yes would lead into a world that would make no sense to my project management software. No would return me to my scheduled life, a life of calibrated light and optimal outcomes. My glasses pulsed. Singapore was waiting.

“Yes,” I said.

The stairwell smelled of damp stone and turmeric. Her apartment was on the fourth floor, no elevator. The door opened not onto a home, but onto a curated collision.

It was a single large room, a loft that seemed to defy its own physical limits. The walls were not walls; they were layered. The bottom layer was exposed brick, then a layer of peeling, water-stained wallpaper showing faint roses, then a layer of corkboard pinned with hundreds of physical photographs, postcards, and scraps of fabric. Above that, suspended from the ceiling on nearly invisible filaments, were dozens of small, clear glass boxes. Inside each was a single, mundane object: a single die, a burnt-out lightbulb, a child’s plastic dinosaur, a key. They hung at different heights, turning slowly in the draft from the door, casting tiny, distorted shadows.

In the centre of the room was a large, scarred wooden table holding a disassembled rotary telephone, a soldering iron, and a terrarium containing a thriving colony of neon-purple mushrooms. One entire corner was a bed, heaped with mismatched blankets and what looked like a deflated inflatable whale. The opposite corner was a kitchenette, every surface covered in clean but chaotic stacks of mismatched china and jars of unidentifiable preserves.

But the dominant feature was the light. She had no overhead lights. Instead, a dozen different sources glowed and flickered: a salt lamp the colour of a dying sun, a string of Edison bulbs draped over a steel beam, a flickering LED panel behind a sheet of frosted plastic that mimicked the pulse of a slow heartbeat, and the cool, blue glow of three large computer monitors stacked vertically on a desk made from a door. On the screens, lines of code scrolled, but also what looked like live feeds of empty park benches, a slowly dripping tap, and a view of the moon through a telescope.

Wren dropped her bag onto a chair that appeared to be made from welded-together bicycle parts. “Welcome to the memory palace. It’s a bit literal.”

I stood just inside the door, my own world feeling thin, weightless. “You built this?”

“Collected it. Curated it. The archive is digital and physical. Symbiotic.” She walked to the desk and tapped a key. The scrolling code vanished, replaced by a grid of thumbnails. She clicked, and the image of the dead pigeon, now looking strangely dignified in its pool of electric light, filled the centre screen. She typed: Subject: Columba livia domestica. Incident: Interrupted pursuit. Location: Crosby & Howard. Collaborator: Leo (ghost class).

“Collaborator?” I asked, moving closer. The air smelled of ozone, solder, and old paper.

“You witnessed. You participated. Your presence is now part of its metadata.” She swivelled the chair to face me. “So. Leo. What do you deliver?”

“I… architect digital experiences. For a firm. We build immersive environments. For brands, mostly.”

“You build haunted houses for products.”

“I suppose.”

“And you live… where? In a white box with a single succulent you forget to water?”

It was so precisely accurate it felt like a violation. “It’s a grey box. And it’s a snake plant.”

She smiled, not unkindly. It was the first real smile, and it transformed her face from a monument to strange grief into something alive, mischievous. “You’re a digital native. I’m a digital surrealist. There’s a difference.”

“Explain.”

“You live in the stream. You are optimized for it. You believe in its reality. I live adjacent to it. I use its tools to document what it ignores. The bleed-through. The glitches. The dead pigeons.” She gestured around the room. “This is all a capture device. For the moments when the simulation stutters.”

My phone vibrated in my pocket. Singapore. I silenced it without looking. The action felt treasonous and exhilarating. “And what do you do with what you capture?”

“I keep it. Sometimes I recombine it. Make new stories from the broken pieces.” She stood and went to the terrarium, tapping the glass. The purple mushrooms seemed to pulse in response. “This is a Psilocybe cubensis variant I’m breeding for heightened visual patterning under specific light frequencies. They’re collaborators, too.”

I was adrift. I was a satellite that had just been grabbed by the gravity of a small, wild planet. “Why did you invite me up here?”

She considered me, her head tilted. The blue streak in her hair fell across one eye. “Because you stopped. In the middle of the stream, you saw a crack in the pavement, and you stopped. That’s a data point. That’s rare.” She walked over to one of the hanging glass boxes, the one with the key, and set it spinning. “And because I’m lonely, Leo. Even an archive needs a visitor sometimes.”

The confession was so blunt it stripped away any possibility of a slick response. I just nodded.

“You can stay,” she said, turning back to her screens. “Or you can go. Your deliverables are calling. But if you stay, there are rules.”

“What rules?”

“No checking your phone in here. The light pollution messes with the ambiance. If you need to speak to someone, use the rotary phone. It only calls one number.”

“What number?”

“Mine. The landline.” She pointed to a vintage pink phone on a shelf by the bed. “And you have to contribute to the archive. Before you leave, you have to give me one thing. A true thing. A fragment. It can be an object, a memory, a sensation. But it has to be true.”

I looked around the room, at the turning glass boxes, the pulsating lights, the scrolling feeds of emptiness. I felt the weight of my own grey life, the sterile timeline of it. Here was texture. Here was decay and strange growth. Here was a woman who mourned pigeons and bred psychedelic fungi and called loneliness by its name.

“I’ll stay,” I said. My voice sounded foreign to me.

“Good.” She didn’t look triumphant, just… satisfied. “The first lesson of surrealism, digital or otherwise: to find the extraordinary, you must first consent to be interrupted.” She tossed me a small, cold object from her pocket. I caught it. It was a smooth, black stone, worn flat by water. “Your token. For the phone. Now, help me with the pigeon’s obituary. I want to get the phrasing right.”

And just like that, I was no longer a ghost in the stream. I was a collaborator. I was in the archive. The world outside the windows—the grid of lights, the silent, rushing data—receded into a faint, irrelevant hum.

Chapter 2

CHAPTER 2

I woke to the sound of rain against glass and the smell of burning toast. My neck was a knot of protest, my spine a series of unhappy opinions. I was on the deflated whale, which was less comfortable than it looked. Grey morning light filtered through the hanging glass boxes, casting moving, liquid shapes across the brick.

Wren was in the kitchenette, wrestling with a toaster that appeared to be from the 1950s, its chrome sides smoking cheerfully. She wore a man’s oversized shirt covered in faded diagrams of constellations.

“It has two settings,” she announced, not turning around. “Pale and vengeful. I chose vengeful.”

I sat up, the events of the previous night reassembling themselves: the pigeon, the stairwell, the archive, the hours spent writing and rewriting a three-line “obituary” for a bird until it felt both utterly ridiculous and profoundly necessary. We’d finally settled on: Here lies a guide who fell from the sky. His map was incomplete. The destination is still pending.

“What time is it?” My voice was gravel.

“Time is a flat circle currently being scorched by this toaster.” She ejected two slices of charcoal, regarded them sadly, then scraped the blackest bits into the sink with a knife. “It’s 7:03 AM. Your glasses have been blinking on the floor for an hour. It was… anxious-making.”

I found them beside the whale. The lenses showed 47 missed notifications, 12 emails flagged urgent, and a calendar alert for a 9 AM project sync I was now, definitively, going to miss. A cold wave of professional dread washed through me. I took them off.

“Rule,” Wren said, pointing the knife at me. “No phantom limb syndrome for the data stream. You’ll have withdrawal. It’s normal. Eat some vengeful toast.”

She handed me a plate. The toast was slathered in butter and a thick, dark purple jam that smelled of violets and something earthier. I took a bite. It was astonishingly good.

“What is this?”

“Elderberry and ghost pipe jelly. From the forage.” She ate standing up, leaning against the counter, watching me. “You slept like you were dead. No dreaming REM flicker under the eyelids. Just… system shutdown. That’s how you people live. It’s not sustainable.”

“You people?”

“The professionally haunted. The ones who outsource their subconscious to cloud servers.” She finished her toast and licked the violet stain from her thumb. “I have a job today. You can come. If you’re not rushing back to your deliverables.”

The challenge was clear. The part of me that was a responsible, leveraged asset screamed to leave, to craft a plausible excuse for last night’s radio silence, to re-enter the stream and swim hard to catch up. But that part was muffled by the taste of ghost pipe jelly and the memory of her typing “Collaborator: Leo (ghost class).”

“What’s the job?”

“I’m a digital funeral director.” At my look, she shrugged. “Freelance. People hire me to curate and then decommission digital estates. Social media, cloud storage, private servers. I find the meaningful fragments, compile a ‘memory core,’ and then perform a deletion ritual. It’s a growth industry. Mortality’s the last big analog experience, but it has a huge digital shadow.”

“You delete people’s lives.”

“I liberate them. The data, I mean. Most of it is noise. Recurring grocery lists. Memes they saved but never sent. Thousands of nearly identical sunset photos. I look for the anomalies. The emails drafted but never sent. The search histories that tell a secret story. The one selfie where they’re truly, unguardedly happy. I save those, give them to the family on an encrypted drive. The rest… I give it a good funeral and pull the plug.”

It was the most morbid, beautiful thing I’d ever heard. “Where is today’s funeral?”

“Upper West Side. A classic six. The deceased was a professor of linguistics. Died three months ago. The family can’t bring themselves to touch his ‘digital dust,’ as they called it. They just want it gone.” She pulled on a pair of boots that looked like they’d been salvaged from a sunken ship. “Grab your coat, ghost. We’re going to perform an exorcism.”

The rain had softened to a mist that hung in the air, turning the city into a blurred impressionist painting. We took the subway, and Wren spent the entire ride staring not at her phone, but at the reflections of people in the dark window opposite, her own face superimposed over theirs. I tried to sit with the silence, with the absence of notifications buzzing against my thigh. It felt like a toothache in my soul.

The professor’s apartment was a time capsule of academia: floor-to-ceiling books, a pervasive smell of pipe tobacco and old paper, an enormous, scarred oak desk. His elderly wife, a small woman with bird-like hands, let us in, her eyes red-rimmed.

“Everything is in his study,” she whispered. “The computer. The little tablets. I don’t know the passwords.”

“We’ll handle it,” Wren said, her voice softer than I’d ever heard it. She placed a hand on the woman’s arm, a gesture of startling tenderness. “Would you like to tell me one thing about him? Something not in any file?”

The woman looked at the closed study door. “He hated the sound of typing. He wrote all his first drafts longhand, with a fountain pen. He said typing sounded like ‘ants marching over the grave of language.’” A faint smile. “He was a silly man.”

“That’s perfect,” Wren said. “Thank you.”

In the study, Wren got to work. She didn’t just sit at the computer. She moved around the room first, running her fingers along the spines of books, opening drawers, smelling the ink in the dried-up fountain pens. She took a small, high-resolution camera from her bag and photographed the desk exactly as it was, the indentations on the leather blotter, the specific arrangement of paperweights.

“Context,” she murmured. “The data doesn’t exist in a vacuum. It lives here.”

Then she sat, plugged a custom external drive into the professor’s ageing desktop, and began to work. Her fingers flew across the keyboard, but this wasn’t mindless scrolling. She used command line interfaces, data-sifting algorithms she’d written herself, her face lit by the cool glow of the monitor. I watched, mesmerized. She was a surgeon in a digital body, carefully separating organs from connective tissue.

“Look here,” she said, not turning. “His search history from the last six months. See the pattern? ‘Synesthesia and phonemes,’ ‘last recorded speaker of Eyak language,’ ‘recipes for oat milk,’ ‘how to tell your wife it’s time.’” She highlighted a cluster of phrases. “The fear and the love, all tangled in the same queries. The profound and the mundane, holding hands.”

She found the fragments. A voice memo of him humming a tune she later identified as a Basque folk song. A scanned drawing, done by a grandchild, labelled “Grandpa’s Words” with colourful squiggles coming out of his mouth. An email draft to an old colleague that contained only the line: “The metaphor has collapsed, Charles. I am living in the rubble and it is more beautiful than the cathedral ever was.”

She saved these to a folder labelled “CORE.” The rest—the gigabytes of PDFs, the administrative emails, the digital receipts—she marked for deletion.

After two hours, she leaned back. “It’s time. The ritual is simple. We acknowledge the weight, then release it.” She opened a terminal window. A string of commands waited, cursor blinking. “Leo, turn off the main light.”

I did. The room was lit only by the desk lamp and the monitor.

Wren placed her hands on the keyboard. She didn’t type immediately. She closed her eyes and took a deep breath, as if listening. Then she spoke, her voice clear in the quiet room.

“Professor Alistair Finch. Your words were your life. These are the echoes we keep.” She tapped a key. On the screen, a progress bar appeared: Deleting 1.4 TB of data. Estimated time: 4 minutes. “The rest we return to the silence from which all language first emerged.”

We watched the bar fill. The hard drive whirred, a sound like a slow exhale. When it finished, the screen went black. Wren unplugged her drive, then gently disconnected the computer’s power cable.

“It’s done.”

The silence in the room felt different. Lighter, but also charged. It was the silence after a story ends.

On the subway back downtown, I finally spoke. “That was… a sacrament.”

“It’s just good digital hygiene,” she said, but she was looking out the window again, her reflection smiling faintly.

“No. It wasn’t. You treated his data like it had a soul.”

“Maybe it does. Maybe a soul is just a particularly dense and meaningful pattern of information.” She turned to me. “Your turn.”

“My turn?”

“The rule. You have to contribute a fragment to the archive. A true thing.”

I panicked. My life felt like a vault of proprietary information and non-disclosure agreements. What true thing did I possess? Then I remembered. It was stupid. It was small.

“When I was nine,” I said, the words coming out before I could stop them, “I had a gerbil named Mercury. He escaped his cage. We found him two days later, behind the refrigerator, alive. But he was cold, so cold. I held him in my cupped hands for an hour, breathing on him, trying to warm him up. He did get warm. And then he died. The warmth didn’t save him; it just… let him finish dying. I’ve never told anyone that.”

The train rattled through a tunnel. The lights flickered.

Wren didn’t say anything for a long moment. Then she reached into her bag, pulled out a small, blank sticker, and wrote on it with a silver pen: Fragment: Leo. The warmth at the end. She stuck it to the metal pole between us. “It’s archived.”

We rode the rest of the way in silence. But it was a different kind of silence than before. It was a shared one. When we got back to Crosby Street, the pavement was dry, the ghost of the pigeon gone. My phone, when I finally looked at it, was a screaming monument to a world that now felt distant and slightly ridiculous.

“You’re going back, aren’t you?” Wren said, reading my face as I stared at the screen.

“I have to. Just to… explain. To manage the fallout.”

She nodded, unsurprised. “The stream has strong currents. It’s hard to climb out.” She dug in her pocket and handed me the smooth black stone. “Your token. In case you want to call. The landline only rings here.”

I took it. It was warm from her body. “Wren… last night. Today. Thank you.”

“Don’t thank me. You paid your entry fee.” She pushed open the heavy door to her building. Before she disappeared inside, she looked back. “A word of advice, Leo. When you’re in there, in your grey box with your snake plant… look for the cracks. The pigeons. They’re everywhere.”

Then she was gone, swallowed by the dark stairwell. I stood on the street, the stone in one hand, my glowing, frantic phone in the other. I was a ghost again. But now I knew I was a ghost. And I had, tucked away in a surrealist’s archive, a fragment that proved, once, I had been real.

Chapter 3

CHAPTER 3

The re-entry was violent.

My apartment wasn’t grey; it was greige. A colour designed to offend no one and nourish nothing. The snake plant in the corner was, indeed, thriving on my neglect. The silence was absolute and sterile. I put my glasses on, and the world exploded into a constellation of demands.

The 9 AM sync had been a disaster in my absence. A client was furious over a delayed asset. My project lead had sent seven increasingly terse messages. The Singapore team had worked through the night and needed immediate feedback. I spent three hours putting out fires, my apologies a smooth, professional paste over the gaping hole of my unexplained absence. I blamed a family emergency, a vague, forgivable sin. The stream accepted the offering, and the current swept me up again.

But I was different. I was a contaminated file.

During a video call about user engagement metrics, I found myself staring at a graph of quarterly growth, its upward trend a perfect, soulless curve, and all I could think was: This is a man who hated the sound of typing. I’d watch people on screens, in meetings, in coffee shops, and see not their avatars, but their potential fragments. The woman nervously twisting her wedding ring: what unsent email did that correspond to? The man laughing too loudly at his own joke: what search history of loneliness hid behind that?

I was haunted by the archive.

I lasted four days. Four days of deliverables, optimized workflows, and the crushing weight of a reality that felt like a cheap simulation. On the fifth evening, staring at a dinner of delivery sushi that tasted of cardboard and regret, I reached into my pocket. The black stone was there, worn smooth by the constant, unconscious friction of my fingers.

I didn’t have a landline. I went to the building’s lobby, an empty, marble space with a single, antique payphone booth that had been preserved as an “art piece.” It didn’t work. Of course it didn’t. I used my phone to look up the number for Wren’s landline, a number I realized I’d never asked for. It was unlisted.

The fragment was going to be lost. The connection, severed. The thought induced a panic more profound than any missed deadline.

I did the only thing I could think of. I went back to Crosby Street. It was past midnight. The city’s glow was a low, electric hum. Her building door was locked. No rusted metal plate for me. I stood there, feeling foolish. Then I remembered the pigeon. The utility door recess.

I walked to the spot. The door was still closed. But tucked into the gap between the door and the frame, almost invisible, was a small, clear glass vial with a cork stopper. Inside was a single, grey feather. Tied to it with a red thread was a tag, and in tiny, precise handwriting, it read: Columbae liviae domesticae – plume de voyage. For the collaborator, in case of return.

She’d known I’d come back.

I took the vial. As I turned, the utility door clicked open. Not the main door, this one. A narrow, dark passage led into the building’s underbelly. I didn’t hesitate.

The passage wound past boilers and electrical conduits, then up a narrow, iron spiral staircase that emerged through a hatch in the floor of Wren’s loft. I pushed it open.

The room was different. The pulsating LED panel was off. The only light came from the salt lamp and the three monitors, which now showed a single, continuous feed: a live camera view from a weather station on a remote cliff in Scotland. Wind lashed rain against the lens in rhythmic, grey waves. The sound filled the room, a roar of static and elemental fury.

Wren was at her desk, but she wasn’t working. She was sitting very still, wrapped in a blanket, staring at the storm on the screens. She didn’t turn as I climbed out.

“You’re late,” she said, her voice flat. “The funeral was yesterday.”

A cold knot formed in my stomach. “What funeral?”

“Mine.” She finally swivelled to face me. Her eyes were the storm on the screen, distant and full of weather. She looked exhausted, hollowed out. “Or, the funeral for a version of me. A major data loss.”

“What happened?”

She gestured to the terrarium. The purple mushrooms were gone. In their place was a grey, fibrous mould, spreading like a blight across the soil. “Contamination. A rival fungus. It got in. It out-competed them. They’re gone.” She said it with the finality of a death sentence. “I’d been cultivating that strain for eighteen months. They were the key to the next phase.”

“The next phase of what?”

“The project. The Catalogue isn’t just an archive. It’s a… translation engine. I use the fragments—the dead pigeons, the unsent emails, the crushed biscuits—as source code. The mushrooms, under the right light, helped me see the patterns, the connections between seemingly random fractures. They were my organic processors.” She looked at the dead terrarium with genuine grief. “Now the processor is dead. The data is just data again. Noise.”

I moved closer, the vial with the feather still in my hand. “I’m sorry, Wren.”

“Don’t be. It’s the violence of the incidental. The casual murder of a narrative.” She was quoting her own words from our first meeting, but now they were drained of passion, just a bleak report. “It happens. I should have built a better firewall. Biological or digital, corruption finds a way.”

The room, usually so full of strange life, felt like a tomb. The turning glass boxes seemed like relics in a museum. I held out the vial. “I found this.”

A flicker in her eyes. The ghost of her smile. “I know. I left it. A 73% probability you’d return. You have the look of someone who just found the crack in their world. It’s addictive.”

“I brought you something else.” I set the vial on her desk. From my backpack, I took out my personal laptop, the one not owned by my company. I opened it, navigated past the familiar icons, and pulled up a folder. “A fragment. For the archive. A true thing.”

It was a folder of video files. Dates from seven years ago. I clicked one. The shaky, low-resolution footage showed a younger me, my face softer, hair longer, laughing. I was in a backyard at night, pointing the camera at a makeshift telescope—a long tube on a tripod. My father’s voice, off-screen, warm and slightly teasing: “You’re pointing it at the neighbour’s bathroom window, Leo.” My laughter, genuine, unselfconscious. “It’s Jupiter, you philistine! I can see the bands! Look!” The video ended as I moved to show him the eyepiece.

“My father,” I said, my throat tight. “He died two years after that. A sudden aneurysm. Digital, but… fast. No time for fragments. I’ve never watched these. They just sat there. Taking up space.”

Wren was staring at the frozen, pixelated image of my laughing face. The storm raged on the screens behind her. Slowly, she reached out and touched the laptop’s screen, her finger over the ghost of my father’s shoulder.

“This is a high-value fragment,” she whispered. “Why give it to me?”

“Because you’ll know what to do with it. You won’t just let it sit in the cloud, decaying. You’ll… make it part of the story.”

She closed the laptop lid gently. For a long moment, she was silent. Then she took a deep, shuddering breath, as if coming up for air. The hollow look began to recede, replaced by a fierce, calculating light.

“The mushrooms are gone,” she said, standing up abruptly. The blanket fell to the floor. “The organic processor is offline. But the data remains. And I have a new collaborator.” She looked at me, and the storm in her eyes was back, but now it was focused, directed. “You’re a digital native. You speak the language of the stream fluently. You can navigate its structures, its systems, in ways I can’t. I can see the cracks, but you can… you can engineer them.”

“What are you saying?”

“I’m saying the project isn’t dead. It’s pivoting.” She began to pace, energy returning to her limbs. “We use your skills. My vision. We don’t just document the interruptions. We orchestrate them. Gently. Ethically. We create moments of surreal, meaningful fracture within the stream itself. Not as vandalism. As… art. As wake-up calls. We use the stream’s own tools to show people the dead pigeons they’re walking past.”

It was insane. It was brilliant. It was a line I could never uncross.

“You’re talking about hacking. About intervention.”

“I’m talking about curation on a live canvas. We find the moments ripe for poetry—a stock trader’s moment of doubt, a dating app conversation turning real, a city-wide system glitch—and we introduce a gentle, beautiful anomaly. A fragment, placed perfectly. To remind them they’re real.” She stopped in front of me. “You said you build haunted houses for products. Help me build one for humanity. A beautiful, gentle haunting.”

The stakes had just escalated from personal fascination to a profound, irreversible complication. This wasn’t observing surrealism. This was committing it. It was a violation of the terms of service of modern life. My professional self screamed in warning. But the ghost, the collaborator, the man who had held a dying gerbil and watched his father laugh through a telescope, leaned forward.

“Show me,” I said. “Show me the first one.”

Her smile was a sunrise after a long, dark night. She went to her desk, entered a series of commands. The Scottish storm vanished. On the screens appeared a real-time map of the city, overlaid with dozens of pulsing data streams—public transit delays, social media sentiment analysis, weather patterns, energy grid load.

“Here,” she said, pointing to a subway station in Brooklyn. “In 47 minutes, the northbound F train will be held for a signal issue. Average delay: 8 minutes. A pocket of forced, shared stillness for approximately 300 people. A crack in the commute.” She pulled up another window: a live, publicly accessible feed from a security camera on the station platform. “We give them a fragment. Something to find in that stillness.”

“What fragment?”

She opened a file on her drive. It was a high-resolution scan of a handwritten note on yellowed paper. The writing was elegant, old-fashioned. It read: My dearest E— The daffodils in the park are an arrogant yellow today. They remind me of your laugh before the war. I am afraid of the silence that comes after the sirens. Always, your H.

“Found it in a book bought at a stoop sale,” Wren said. “Never sent. Or never arrived. A love letter from a ghost.” She looked at me. “Your turn. You know the systems. How do we get this fragment onto that platform, in that moment, in a way that feels like magic, not malware?”

I looked at the map, the data streams, the live feed. My mind, trained for seamless user experience, began to work the problem. Not to sell anything. Not to optimize. To give. To interrupt.

“The station has free public Wi-Fi,” I said slowly, thinking it through. “With a splash page for terms of service. During the delay, we could temporarily hijack that splash page. Not to take over, just to… layer. A transparent overlay. The note appears, fades after 30 seconds. No links, no branding. Just the fragment. It’ll look like a glitch. A beautiful glitch.”

Wren’s eyes were wide. “Can you do that?”

“I know people who can. I know the protocols. It’s a minor, temporary DNS redirect. It’s… it’s possible.” The technical path was unfolding in my mind, a ghostly blueprint. “But Wren… if we get caught. It’s not just a violation. It’s a crime.”

“So is forgetting you’re alive,” she said softly. She placed her hand over mine on the desk. Her skin was warm. “This is the complication, Leo. The first major one. You can walk back into the greige. Save the fragments for yourself. Or you can help me plant them in the world. What do you deliver?”

I looked at our hands. At the screens showing the pulsing, anxious city. At the dead terrarium and the live, stormy hope in her face. I saw my father’s ghost in the telescope lens. I felt the cold body of the gerbil growing warm in my hands.

I made my choice.

“I deliver experiences,” I said. And for the first time, the words meant what they were supposed to mean. “Let’s build a haunted house.”

Chapter 4

CHAPTER 4

The historian lived in a book. Or rather, a brownstone in the West Village had been consumed by one, digested slowly over decades until the walls were just thicker spines, the windows mere gaps in the text. I stood on the stoop, the iron railings cold under my palm, and felt the silence. It wasn’t the greige silence of my apartment, that dead, neutral hum. This was a held breath. The kind of quiet that follows the last note of a song you wish hadn’t ended.

I’d found him through layers of digital sediment—obscure forum posts from the early 2000s, citations in dissertations no one read, a whisper in the footnotes of a PDF about civic decay in pre-war Paris. Dr. Alistair Vance. He hadn’t published in fifteen years. His university profile was a ghost page, a 404 error wearing academic robes. But Wren, with her instinct for the buried thing, had unearthed a current address. “He’s either our best chance,” she’d said, handing me a slip of paper with the street name, “or a very elegant dead end. There’s a difference, you know, between being forgotten and hiding.”

I pressed the bell. No sound echoed within. I was about to press again when the intercom crackled, a dry, papery sound.

“Yes.”

Not a question. A statement of fact, weary and final.

“Dr. Vance? My name is Leo. I was given your name by… by someone who thinks you might understand fragments.”

A longer crackle. The sigh of static. “I understand dust. Go away.”

“It’s about the Caelum Society.”

The silence that followed was different. Sharper. A needle dropped in a library. The door buzzed, a sound like a dying wasp, and clicked open.

The foyer was a canyon of paper. Towers of books, manuscripts in teetering piles, folios spilling their guts across a faded Turkish rug. The air smelled of binding glue and ozone, and underneath it, the faint, sweet rot of apples. A single path wound through the chaos, worn into the floorboards. I followed it, my shoulder brushing against a stack of National Geographics from the 1970s. They didn’t wobble. They were part of the architecture.

He was at the end of the path, in a chair that might have once been leather but was now mostly memory. He was older than I expected, not in years but in texture. His face was a well-folded map, his eyes the clear, pale blue of a winter morning sky—the kind that promises cold, not clarity. He wore a cardigan with leather patches on the elbows, the wool fraying into a soft halo. In his lap was a book, but it was closed, his finger marking a place.

“You said Caelum,” he said. His voice was quieter in person, a rustle of pages. “A word not spoken by anyone with a functioning brainstem in at least two decades. Who gave you my name?”

“A friend. She finds things.”

“She finds trouble. Sit. If you can find a surface not dedicated to a more useful purpose.”

I moved a stack of letters tied with baker’s twine from a wooden stool and sat. The stool creaked under me, a protest.

“The Caelum Society,” I began. “I have a… a fragment. Something they made. Or left behind. I need to understand what it is.”

He watched me. It wasn’t a hostile look, nor a curious one. It was an assessment, like a geologist judging the strike of a rock. “The Caelum Society didn’t make artifacts, Mr. Leo. They curated moments. They were librarians of experience. Archivists of the ephemeral. What most people call ‘art’ was, to them, a clumsy first draft. They were interested in the thing that happens between the art and the person witnessing it. The spark in the synapse. The catch in the breath.” He leaned forward slightly. “They believed the most profound human experiences were non-transferable. Un-recordable. You could only set the stage for them, and then… get out of the way.”

I thought of the note we’d planted. I am afraid of the silence that comes after the sirens. A stage set in a subway delay.

“My fragment,” I said. “It’s a viewing device. A telescope, but… not for stars.”

“Describe it.”

I did. The cold brass, the weight, the lenses that seemed to drink the light. The way it showed not what was, but what had been felt. The ghost in the glass.

When I finished, he was very still. The only movement was the slow, deliberate blink of his pale eyes. “You’ve seen through it.”

“Yes.”

“And what did you deliver, Mr. Leo? A report? A valuation? A user-testing summary?”

The question was a scalpel. It found the space between my ribs where my old life still lived. “I delivered an experience,” I said, and the words, for the second time, felt true, but also fragile here, in this mausoleum of knowledge.

A faint, dry smile touched his lips. “Ah. You’re one of hers. I should have known. The last romantic. She believes in haunting the living. I believe in documenting the dead. It’s a fundamental disagreement.” He opened the book in his lap. It was a journal, bound in scuffed green cloth. “What you have is not a telescope. That’s a child’s name for it. The Caelum called it an Anamnesis. From the Greek. Not simply ‘memory.’ The opposite of amnesia. The act of un-forgetting. Of re-collecting the scattered pieces of a lived truth.”

Anamnesis. The word settled in me, heavy and right. A name for the thing.

“They built only a handful,” Vance continued, tracing a line of text with a bony finger. “Each one was keyed, they believed, to a specific strand of human sentiment. A specific loss, or longing. Yours… the description of the cold brass, the lunar association… it might be keyed to Selenelapse. The loss specific to time. Not the passing of it, but the erosion of a particular moment. The memory of a specific moonlight on a specific face, worn smooth by all the other moons that came after.”

My father’s face in the lens. Not as he was at the end, but in the middle, when he was just my father. The moment before the erosion.

“Where did it come from?” I asked.

“That,” he said, closing the book with a soft thump, “is a different question. And the answer is not a place on a map. It’s a pattern. The Caelum didn’t believe in vaults or museums. They believed in seeding. They would place an object—an Anamnesis, a coded journal, a prepared specimen—into the stream of the world. Into an estate sale. A library discard pile. A forgotten storage unit. They trusted the right fragment would find the right person, at the right moment of need. It was a form of faith. Delusional, of course.”

“But you kept their records.”

“I keep all records. The difference between a historian and a hoarder is a thesis statement.” He gestured to the chaos around us. “This is my Anamnesis. A device for un-forgetting a world that prefers to wipe itself clean every morning. For the Caelum, I have… clues. Traces. They communicated through ciphers and coincidence. To find where your device was meant to lead, you don’t need an address. You need a key.”

He stood, his joints issuing soft protests. He moved to a tall, narrow cabinet, its wood dark with age, and unlocked it with a small key from his pocket. Inside were not books, but boxes. Flat, long boxes of the kind used for archival storage. He selected one, brought it back, and laid it on a small cleared space on his desk. The lid lifted with a whisper of released air.

Inside, on a bed of acid-free paper, lay a journal. It was small, bound in what looked like water-stained vellum, tied shut with a black cord. Next to it was a single, tarnished brass key, simple and old.

“This,” he said, “is the diary of Elara Voss. A minor poet, a major depressive. And, for a time, a Caelum aspirant. She never made it past the periphery. Her heart was too loud, her mind too messy for their clinical precision. She loved one of them. It didn’t end well. This journal is her attempt to understand their work, to prove her worth. It is, consequently, the most human record of them that exists. Full of errors, and emotion, and therefore… truth.”

He didn’t hand it to me. He placed his hand on the cover. “She writes of a ‘Selenic Lens’ being prepared for ‘the keeper of the tidal memory.’ It’s your Anamnesis. She writes that its counterpart—its destination—was to be placed ‘where the bridge watches its own ghost.’ That is your key. Not that,” he nodded at the brass key in the box. “That is for a door that no longer stands. The phrase. ‘Where the bridge watches its own ghost.’”

I turned the phrase over in my mind. It felt like a riddle from Wren. A beautiful glitch in meaning.

“Why give this to me?”

“Because you are already in the stream, Mr. Leo. You have the device. You have the romantic. You are past the point of safe observation. You are a participant. And this journal,” he tapped the vellum cover, “is a participant’s record. It belongs out there, where things are felt. Not in here, where things are known.” He finally lifted his hand. “Take it. And the key. A relic for a relic.”

I reached for the journal. As my fingers touched the cool, stiff cover, the front window of the brownstone exploded.

Not with a dramatic, cinematic crash, but with a sickening, wet punch of glass and wood and lead. A heavy beanbag round, the kind used for breaching, tore through the bookshelf beside Vance’s head, showering the room with a blizzard of shredded paper and splinters. The silence was murdered. Replaced by a ringing, dusty roar.

Vance didn’t cry out. He flinched, a full-body contraction, and his eyes met mine. In them was no surprise, only a profound, weary resignation. See? that look said. This is what happens to the remembered.

A voice, electronically amplified, blunt as a hammer, came from the street. “Alistair Vance. Leo Gault. Exit the premises with your hands visible. Now.”

They knew my name. The greige had a name for me.

Vance was moving, faster than I thought possible. He snatched the brass key from the box, pressed it and the journal into my hands, and shoved me toward the back of the canyon. “Through the kitchen. Down the garden steps. The alley gate is rusted but it opens. Go.”

“They’ll take you.”

“They’ll take my papers. They’ll take my quiet. They will not get the thing they actually want, because I don’t have it.” He gave me another shove. “You do. Now go. The bridge that watches its own ghost. Remember it.”

Another round slammed into the front door, buckling the heavy wood. The electronic voice began a countdown from ten.

I ran. I stumbled down the narrow path, past the kitchen with its single, clean cup in the sink, and out a creaking back door into a postage-stamp garden. Dead ivy clawed at a brick wall. Stone steps led down to a moss-slick alley. I fumbled with the latch on the wrought-iron gate; it screamed like a living thing, but gave. I spilled into the alley, the journal clutched to my chest like a stolen heart.

I heard the front door give way. A crash of falling literature. Muffled shouts. No gunshots. Just the violent, efficient sound of containment.

I ran. Not with direction, but with the pure, animal logic of the hunted. I cut across streets, through the skeletal gardens of other brownstones, my breath sawing in my throat, my glasses smeared with the mist of my own panic. I didn’t stop until I was deep inside the labyrinth of the Meatpacking District, under the rusted steel bones of the High Line, the old freight rail line turned elevated park.

I leaned against a cold concrete pillar, sucking in air that tasted of diesel and damp earth. My hands were shaking. I looked down. The vellum journal was warm from my grip. The brass key dug into my palm.

They knew my name. The opposition was not a concept. It was a team with breaching rounds and electronic voices. It was real, and it was here, and it had just swallowed Dr. Alistair Vance and his quiet canyon of dust.

I looked up, through the steel grates of the High Line above me. People walked up there, laughing, taking pictures of the art installations, living in the curated present. And below, in the shadows, I held a fragment of a forgotten past, and a key to a ghost.

Where the bridge watches its own ghost.

My phone buzzed in my pocket. A single message from Wren.

The splash page glitch worked. For 30 seconds, 1,200 people saw a love letter from a ghost. Then it vanished. The platform is buzzing about a ‘poetry hack.’ No traces. You delivered.

I stared at the words. We had planted a fragment. We had made a beautiful glitch. And in doing so, I had painted a target on my back, and on the back of an old man in a book-cave.

The phone buzzed again. Another message, this one from an unknown number. Just an image.

It was a photograph, taken from street level, of this very alley. Of me, right now, hunched and panting against the pillar. The timestamp was one minute ago.

Below the image, a line of text appeared, then vanished as the message deleted itself.

We deliver experiences, too, Leo. What’s your next move?

I looked up, scanning the rooftops, the windows, the dark spaces between the steel beams above. I saw no one. Only the city, watching itself. A bridge to nowhere, watching its own ghost. And I, a fragment in its hand.

Chapter 5

CHAPTER 5

The bus to the coast smelled of damp wool and diesel, a rolling tomb with fogged windows. I’d bought the ticket with cash from a machine that wheezed like an asthmatic, my fingers leaving no smudge on the touchscreen. My phone was a brick in my backpack, battery dead, SIM card floating somewhere in the Hudson. I was a cut thread. The ghost in the stream had evaporated.

Wren’s last message was a fossil in my mind. You delivered. So had they. The image of my own hunched back, the text that dissolved. An experience, delivered. I watched the city peel away through the grimy glass, not elegantly, not with a skyline postcard farewell. It bled out into strip malls and scrapyards, into marshes choked with plastic bags snagged on reeds like shameful flags. The raw, unmediated grief was gone. In its place was a hollow, a canyon of dust inside my own ribs. I’d wept for a bird. Who would weep for the old man in his book-cave? For me, if I vanished into the geology of this pursuit?

I slept in fits, my head against the cold window, the vellum journal a hard knot against my sternum. The key was on a string around my neck now, a cold tooth against my skin. Each jolt of the bus made it bite.

The town, when I stumbled out into it seven hours later, wasn’t a town. It was a shingle of weather-beaten wood and salt-corroded concrete clinging to the cliff’s edge like a limpet. The air didn’t taste of diesel here. It tasted of rot and salt and a cold so profound it felt like the absence of time. The sky was the color of a bruise healing to grey. The wind was a constant narration, a hiss off the Atlantic that said gone, gone, gone.

I was late. I was always late. But for what, now? The schedule was dust. The opposition was a team with a sense of theatricality and a sniper’s eye. My plan, scrawled on a napkin and memorized before I ate it, was a child’s map to a phantom treasure. Find the guide. Find the cave. Find the first trial. The journal’s clue was a fragment of a fragment: Where the bridge watches its own ghost. I’d thought it was the High Line. I was wrong. It was literal.

The bridge was a quarter-mile down the coast, visible from the single main street. A skeletal, rusted railroad trestle, long severed on both ends, jutting out over the violent water. It went nowhere. It was a bridge to its own impossibility. And beneath it, the water foamed and crashed, a perpetual, churning ghost of its own passage.

My instructions were to wait at the Fisherman’s Last, a pub whose sign showed a drowned man smiling peacefully as bubbles rose from his boots. Inside, it was dark and warm with the smell of fried food and peat smoke. Three old men at the bar turned as one when I entered, their conversations dying. Their eyes were like stones smoothed by the sea. They saw the city on me, the panic still in the set of my shoulders, the way my glasses were clean now but my hands wouldn’t stay still.

I ordered a whiskey I didn’t want. The barman, a man with forearms like knotted rope, slid it over without a word. I took it to a corner table slick with condensation, my back to the wall. The napkin under the glass had a lighthouse printed on it, faded by washings. I traced the spiral of the lighthouse stair. A path upward, into light. A path I was walking in reverse, down into the dark.

She came in an hour later. Not with a bang, not with a dramatic gust of wind. The door just opened, and the sound of the sea grew louder for a second, and then she was inside, shaking the weather from her oilskin coat like a dog. She was maybe sixty, maybe seventy, her face a topography of wind and sun, her hair a wild grey crest under a knitted cap. She didn’t look at the old men. She looked directly at me.

She walked over, pulled out the chair opposite with a scrape that set my teeth on edge, and sat. She placed her hands on the table. They were a map of scars, nicks, and calluses, the skin permanently stained with something dark. Engine grease, or peat.

“You’re Leo,” she said. Her voice was the wind given words, rasping and low.

“I was told to ask for Elspeth.”

“You just did.” She didn’t smile. Her eyes, the color of the sea under cloud, scanned my face. “You look like you’ve been carrying a dead thing.”

The metaphor was so blunt it was physical. I felt the weight of the journal, the key. “I have.”

“Pigeon or person?”

The air left my lungs. I took off my glasses, wiped them on my shirt though they were clean. A stalling gesture. The raw grief on Crosby Street, the catastrophic event. It felt like a story about someone else. “Does it matter?”

“To the pigeon? To the person? Yeah. To me? Only for the telling.” She signaled the barman, who brought her a mug of black tea without being asked. “Vance sent a message. Before he went quiet. Said a city boy with a ghost in his pocket might come looking for the first door. Said to help you if you made it this far. If you were stupid enough to still be carrying the ghost.” She sipped her tea. “Are you stupid, Leo?”

The question wasn’t an insult. It was a diagnostic. “Probably. The opposition has pictures. They know my face.”

“The ‘opposition’.” She repeated the word like it was a foreign delicacy she found distasteful. “Here, we just call them the Company. They’ve got a research station ten miles up the coast. Seismic surveys, acoustic mapping. Looking for oil, gas, rare earths. Nosing around the old places. They bought the mayor. They bought the pub, too, but Tom here,” she jerked a thumb at the barman, “forgot to tell them he owns the land under it. Lease is up in six months. He’s a holdout.”

I looked at Tom, who was polishing a glass, his face impassive. A holdout. I was in a town of holdouts, and I was the incoming liability. “I don’t want trouble.”

Elspeth barked a laugh, a short, dry sound. “Trouble’s not a want. It’s a weather. It comes in. You batten down or you drown. You’ve brought a squall with you, city boy. I can smell it on you. The panic. The running.”

I had no rebuttal. The whiskey was a fire in my empty gut. “Vance’s message. Did he say anything else?”

“He said you had a journal. That it points to the Trial of the Witness. First of three. He said the Witness isn’t a thing you find. It’s a thing you become.” She finished her tea. “He also said you’re good with systems. With making beautiful glitches.” Her sea-eyes fixed on me. “The love letter from the ghost. That was you?”

I nodded, once.

“Pretty,” she said, and there was no compliment in it. “A pretty splash. Like a dying fish slapping the water. Gets attention. From everyone.” She stood, pulling her oilskin back on. “Sunset’s in forty minutes. That’s when the bridge lines up. You want to see the door? Come now. Or finish your drink and get the next bus back to the world of vibrations in your palm. Your choice.”

She was already at the door. I left the whiskey half-finished, the lighthouse napkin smeared with wet rings. I followed her into the wind.

She didn’t speak as we walked. The path was not a path. It was a scramble over slick, black rocks, through tufts of tough grass that cut at my hands. The wind was a physical adversary, shoving me towards the cliff edge where the Atlantic churned, white and furious, hundreds of feet below. Elspeth moved over the terrain like a thought, effortless, her boots finding purchase where I saw only slickness.

We were headed for the severed bridge. Up close, it was a monster of corrosion. Its iron bones were weeping rust, staining the cliff face like old blood. The roar of the water through the stone pillars below was a constant, low thunder.

“The Company,” she said, not turning, her words ripped from her mouth and handed to me by the wind, “they think it’s about resources. Minerals. Energy. They map the skin. They don’t believe in the marrow.”

“What’s the marrow?” I yelled back.

“Story,” she said, as if it were obvious. “Memory. The weight of a place.”

We reached the base of the cliff where the first massive concrete pillar of the bridge was anchored. It was embedded in the living rock, covered in graffiti that had itself faded to ghosts of color. Elspeth didn’t pause. She led me around to the seaward side, where the spray from below misted the air. There, almost invisible, was a crack in the rock, disguised by a fold in the cliff and a hanging curtain of tough, salty ivy.

“The bridge doesn’t watch from above,” she said, pulling the ivy aside. The opening was just wider than my shoulders. A darkness breathed out from it, cold and smelling of wet stone and age. “It watches from here. The door’s inside. This is where you see its ghost.”

She took a heavy flashlight from her coat and handed it to me. “My part’s done. Vance paid in advance. In books. First editions he shouldn’t have parted with.” For the first time, something softened in her face, a fracture of human feeling. “He was a good man. His quiet canyon. I hope he’s at peace in it.”

“They took him,” I said, the words raw.

“I know.” She looked at me, and her gaze was no longer diagnostic. It was pitying. “That’s the mortality of it, Leo. We love things—people, ideas, ghosts—and they are perishable. The love isn’t. The love is the fossil left in the rock after the bone is gone. That’s the weight. That’s the marrow. Now, you go in. The alignment lasts ten minutes at sunset. The light hits a specific seam inside. It shows the way. After that, you’re on your own.”

“What do I do?”

“You witness.” She stepped back. “And Leo? Don’t drop your phone in there. No service anyway.”

I switched on the flashlight. The beam was a solid thing in the damp air. I looked at the crack, at the darkness that had been waiting. I thought of the curated present of the High Line, the people laughing above me while I held a fragment below. Now there was no above. There was only below.

I didn’t say goodbye. I turned sideways and shuffled into the rock.

The cave was not a cave. It was a throat.

The walls, once I was through the entrance, closed not in, but down. The floor sloped sharply, a ramp of smooth, water-worn stone descending into the belly of the cliff. My flashlight beam danced over striations of rock—grey, black, a vein of startling white quartz that gleamed like a nerve. The sound of the sea was muffled here, replaced by the drip of water and the roar of my own blood in my ears. The air was cold enough to ache.

I descended for what felt like years, the ramp twisting, the ceiling sometimes soaring into invisible blackness, sometimes pressing down so low I had to crawl. The journal was a live coal against my chest. I was following the directions Vance had pieced together, the clues in the vellum: Descend the watching bridge’s root. Seek the blind eye that weeps light.

The ramp ended in a chamber. It was circular, perhaps thirty feet across, the ceiling a dome lost in shadow. In the center was a still, black pool of water, fed by a single, slow drip from above. Plink. Plink. A millennia-long metronome. And on the far side of the pool, carved directly into the rock face, was a door.

It was simple. Rectangular. Seven feet high, three wide. It had no handle, no keyhole. Its surface was not smooth; it was covered in shallow, intricate carvings, swirls and interlocking lines that hurt my eyes to follow. They looked less like decoration and more like circuitry, or the mycelial maps of some vast, subterranean fungus.

This was it. The first trial site. The door had no business being here, in this impossible chamber beneath a severed bridge. It was a sheer act of will made stone.

I swung my flashlight beam around, looking for the “blind eye.” The walls were rough, natural. Then the beam caught it. High on the wall to the left of the door, a smooth, hemispherical depression about the size of a dinner plate. It was filled with a substance that wasn’t rock—it was opaque, milky, like a cataract.

The blind eye that weeps light.

Sunset. The alignment.

I checked my watch. Two minutes. I stood there, in the cathedral quiet, the drip the only sound. I thought of mortality. Of Alistair Vance, whose love for forgotten stories was now a fossil in the world. Of the weeping woman and her pigeon, a love so vast it encompassed the irrelevant dead. Of Wren, whose loyalty was a glitch in the system of a cynical world. Of the love I had for this puzzle, this ghost—a love that had led me here, to a door in the dark, possibly to my own perishable end.

The beam from my flashlight began to change. A faint, ruddy glow was seeping into the chamber from the entrance passageway behind me. The sunset. The light was traveling down the throat of the cave, finding its path.

I switched off my flashlight. The darkness was absolute, a velvet weight. Then the glow grew, a faint orange thread becoming a river. It painted the chamber in monochrome relief. It climbed the far wall.

With a silent, profound grace, the beam of dying sunlight found the blind eye.

The milky substance in the depression didn’t clear. It activated. It began to glow from within, a soft, phosphorescent blue. The light wasn’t a reflection; it was a weeping, a slow, liquid radiance that spilled down the wall. It traced the ancient carvings on the door, filling the grooves with cold fire.

The circuitry lit up.

It was a map. A sentence. A song. The light flowed through the channels, connecting symbols, revealing a logic that was both mathematical and poetic. I saw a pattern that echoed the spiral on the pub napkin. I saw a representation of the bridge above. I saw a human figure, arms outstretched, standing before a smaller, darker door within the larger one.

The light reached the bottom of the door and pooled there. With a sound like a sigh, a section of the rock floor in front of the door—a slab I hadn’t distinguished from the rest—sank down an inch and slid sideways.

Revealing a narrow, vertical shaft. A descent within the descent.

The weeping light from the eye began to fade. The sunset alignment was passing. In moments, the chamber would be plunged back into total black.

This was the point of no return. The entrance was a quarter-mile of twisting, climbing darkness behind me. The shaft was here, now, offered by the ghost of the sun.

I thought of the message from the unknown number. We deliver experiences. This was mine. To witness. To become the witness.

I took the key from around my neck. The brass was warm from my skin. There was no keyhole. But it was the token, the proof of my stupid, mortal love for the ghost.

As the last of the blue light died, leaving only the faintest afterglue on my retinas, I heard it. From the black pool in the center of the chamber.

A splash.

Not the plink of a drip. A purposeful, muscular displacement of water.

I froze, my breath caught in my throat. I fumbled for my flashlight, my fingers numb. I clicked it on.

The beam stabbed across the pool.

The water was still, black, glassy. Ripples expanded from the center, lapping against the stone edge.

Something had surfaced. Or submerged.

I swept the beam around the chamber. Nothing. The door was just a door again, its carvings inert. The shaft yawned at my feet.

Then the drip. Plink.

And a voice. Not from the shaft. Not from the door.

From the pool.

It was a wet, hollow, echoing sound, as if spoken from a great depth through a tube of water. It was a voice that had never learned human language properly, but had absorbed the sounds of the cave—the drip, the sigh of shifting stone, the distant crush of the sea.

It spoke a single sentence, and the words were made of cold water and crushing pressure.

“You carry the love. Now feel the mortality.”

The light behind me—the last of the sunset—snuffed out.

The entrance, with a grinding shriek of stone on stone that vibrated through the soles of my boots, sealed shut.

The darkness was complete. It was a solid thing. It was the inside of a coffin buried at sea.

I was in the throat, and it had swallowed.

My flashlight beam trembled over the now-solid wall where the entrance had been. No crack. No seam. Just immutable rock.

The point of no return wasn’t the shaft.

It was the sound of the seal.

It was the voice from the pool.

I was alone with a ghost that was not in a journal, not in a key, but was the water and the stone and the dark itself. The opposition, the Company, their breaching rounds and electronic surveillance—they were a problem of the world above. This was a problem of the world below.

The beam of my flashlight found the shaft again. The only way was down.

We deliver experiences, too, Leo.

The hook in the dark was not curiosity. It was not a desire for answers. It was the simple, animal need to get out of this chamber, away from the black water and the thing that spoke in it.

I sat on the edge of the shaft, my legs dangling into nothing. The air rising from it was colder, and carried a new smell—not wet stone, but dry, ancient dust, and something else. Something metallic, like old blood, or rust, or both.

I had come to witness.

The ghost in the pool had witnessed me first.

I pushed off, and let the dark take me down.

Chapter 6

CHAPTER 6

The dark didn’t take me so much as I fell into its mouth. The shaft was a vertical throat of polished stone, not rough-hewn but worn smooth by something other than water. My gloves squealed against the sides, friction burning through the synthetic material, the taste of ozone and my own panic sharp in the back of my throat. I dropped for a count of seven—a small eternity measured in hammering heartbeats—before my boots hit uneven ground with a jarring crack that shot up my spine.

The beam of my flashlight, a frantic white finger in the absolute black, skittered across the walls. I was in a chamber no larger than a freight elevator. The air was different here. The wet, organic rot of the pool chamber was gone, replaced by that dry, metallic dust I’d smelled from above. It coated my tongue, tasted of forgotten coins and desiccated insect wings.

The walls were not blank. They were inscribed.

Not with words. With patterns. A chaos of intersecting lines, arcs, and dots carved with a precision that seemed to hum against the light. It looked like the scribbled margin notes of a mad astronomer. I traced a gloved finger over one series of dots. They were drilled, each a perfect hemisphere, cold to the touch.

My phone was dead weight. No signal, not even a ghost of one. The world above—the Company, the Clarion, the whole frantic, vibrating mess of it—felt like a story I’d been told once and half-remembered. This was the reality: the dust, the silent patterns, and the memory of a voice made of water and pressure.

You carry the love. Now feel the mortality.

The love was the journal in my pack, the key against my chest. The mortality was the throb in my knees from the fall, the finite charge in my flashlight, the absolute, sealing dark at my back.

I was here to witness. So I witnessed.

The pattern wasn’t random. The dots, I realized after five minutes of breath-held study, were stars. The lines connected them into constellations, but not any constellations I knew. They were foreign, alien, a sky seen from another angle of the earth. And carved at the base of each wall, almost obscured by grit, was a shallow, circular depression.

A puzzle. Of course it was a puzzle. The ghost wasn’t just a presence; it was a curator. A tester.

I shrugged off my pack, the sound obscenely loud. I pulled out the journal, its leather cover feeling like skin in the cold. Flipping past Elara’s sketches of shorebirds and tide pools, I found the later pages. The frantic ones. The ones that stopped being about observation and started being about navigation.

There, amidst her spiraling script, was a diagram. A series of seven dots, connected by a specific, looping line. The Shepherd’s Crook, she’d labeled it. Seen only in the deep month, an hour before true dawn. The key turns in the lock of night.

It matched one of the patterns on the wall to my left.

The depressions at the base of each wall were the same diameter as the lens of my flashlight.

This wasn’t a lock that required a key. It required a specific light. A specific alignment.

I moved to the wall with the Shepherd’s Crook. My hands were steady now. This was a problem. Problems could be solved. I positioned the beam of my flashlight into the depression. The light shot through it, not a diffuse glow, but a focused, needle-thin ray that lanced across the chamber and hit a corresponding depression on the opposite wall. A soft, resonant thrum vibrated through the stone underfoot.

One.

I found the next pattern from Elara’s journal—The Drowned Lyre—on the right-hand wall. Repeated the process. Another thrum, deeper this time. The dust on the floor stirred.

The third pattern, The Broken Crown, was on the ceiling. I had to brace my back against the wall, my boots scrambling for purchase, to angle the light up into its depression. The beam connected. The thrum was a bass note I felt in my teeth.

On the fourth alignment—The Sighing Sisters—I realized the trap.

It wasn’t about getting it right. It was about timing. The beam had to be placed and held for a precise count, the duration implied by the spacing of the stars in Elara’s sketches. I’d rushed the third one. Held it too long.

A sound like grinding teeth issued from the walls. From a seam I hadn’t seen, a fine, glittering sand began to pour into the chamber from all sides. It wasn’t sand. It was ground glass, or crystalline silica. It hissed as it fell, piling around my ankles. In a closed space this small, it would fill my lungs, scour them to ribbons. It was a death of a thousand tiny cuts, slow and suffocating.

Feel the mortality.

I choked back a cough, my mind white with static. Then I forced it to clear. Elara hadn’t just drawn the patterns. She’d written sequences. Numbers. 3. 7. 2. 5.

Durations.

I had three walls and the ceiling activated. The sand was at my shins, whispering, insistent. I lunged for the final wall, the pattern she’d called The Anchorite’s Gaze. I shoved the flashlight into the depression, holding it firm.

One. Two. Three. Four. Five.

On five, I yanked it free.

The grinding ceased. The sand stopped falling. For a suspended second, nothing. Then, with a sigh of ancient, counter-weighted stone, a section of the wall opposite the shaft slid sideways, revealing a low, dark passage. The air that sighed out was colder still, and carried the faint, distant scent of ozone and stone-dry moss.

I stumbled into the passage, brushing the glittering death-sand from my legs. The door slid shut behind me, sealing with a soft, final click. The ghost had tested my witness. I had passed. For now.

The passage sloped downwards, a serpent’s coil through the bedrock. The walls here were alive with bioluminescent fungi, a faint, blue-green glow that provided just enough light to see the rough floor. It felt like walking through the veins of the earth. The silence was a pressure in my ears.

After an hour of descent, the passage opened into a cavern so vast my flashlight beam dissolved into the gloom before finding a far wall. Stalactites hung like stone teeth, and a forest of stalagmites rose from the floor in chaotic, gnarled formations. A narrow path, worn smooth by time or something else, wound between them.

I was halfway across when I heard the crack.

It wasn’t stone settling. It was a sharp, percussive snap, like a giant bone breaking. It came from above and to my right. I froze, my beam spearing upwards.

A section of the cavern ceiling, a massive dagger of limestone the size of a car, was shearing away from its moorings. And directly beneath its shadow, crouched behind a stalagmite, was a figure.

Human.

They hadn’t seen it. They were focused on something in their hands, a small device with a faintly glowing screen.

There was no time to yell. Only to move.

I was already running, my pack thumping against my back. The world narrowed to the path, the figure, the descending mass of stone. I hit them in a diving tackle, my shoulder driving into their ribs, carrying us both in a tumbling, painful roll behind a broader column of rock.

The impact was a physical shockwave. The sound was beyond deafening—a world-ending CRUMP that slammed into my chest, followed by a hailstorm of shrapnel and a choking cloud of rock dust. The ground jumped. My teeth clacked together. For a moment, there was only the roar and the dust.

Then, a ringing silence.

I was sprawled on top of the stranger. A woman. She was coughing, a raw, ragged sound. My flashlight was gone, lost in the fall. The only light now was the feeble blue-green glow from the fungi, illuminating the dust motes dancing in a mad whirl.

She shoved me off, scrambling to her knees. “What the hell was that?” Her voice was a rasp, sharp with adrenaline and accusation.

“The ceiling,” I coughed, pushing myself up. Every part of me ached. “A piece of it fell.”

“I know what it was.” She found her device, its screen now cracked but still glowing. Its light painted her face in harsh, angular shadows. She was maybe my age, with a sharp, intelligent face currently smudged with grime and blood from a cut on her temple. Her eyes were the kind of dark that absorbs light. She wore practical, worn gear, but it wasn’t Company issue. It was too personalized, patched in places. A seeker. Like me, but not like me. “Why did you tackle me?”

“It was that or let you become a stain.”

She stared at me, her breath pluming in the cold air. The accusation in her eyes didn’t fade, but it was joined by a reluctant, furious calculation. She looked past me at the new mountain of rubble that now dominated the cavern floor, blocking the path I’d come from and half the path ahead. Her intended route was buried under ten tons of stone.

“Mara,” she said finally, not offering a hand. “And you’ve just complicated my exit strategy.”

“Alex.” I found my flashlight a few feet away, miraculously intact. Its beam cut through the settling dust. “I’ve just guaranteed you still have an exit strategy that doesn’t involve being pulp.”

She stood, brushing dust from her trousers with quick, irritated slaps. “I had it under control.”

“You were reading a map.”

“It’s a sonographic imager. It reads density differentials in the rock. I was assessing stability.” She said it with the crisp precision of someone used to being the smartest person in any room, even a collapsing cave. “That fall wasn’t natural. The frequency was wrong. It was triggered.”

“By what?”

“By us. Or by you.” She looked me up and down, her gaze lingering on my Company-issued boots, my pack. “You’re with them. The collectors.”

“I’m a witness.”

She barked a short, humorless laugh. It echoed in the cavern. “A witness. Right. And I’m a tourist. You’re here to extract. To take the experience, bottle it, and sell it to the highest bidder in your curated, sanitized little boxes.”

Her words were needles. They found the bruise of my own doubt. “It’s not that simple.”

“It never is with you people. You think the secret of this place is a thing to be owned. A prize.” She shouldered a small, technical pack. “That collapse was a perimeter alarm. A big one. We’ve tripped it. They’ll know.”

“They?”

But I already knew. The beam of my flashlight swept past her, catching a glint of something in the rubble. Not natural stone. A shard of black composite material. And beside it, a twisted piece of metal with a distinct, helical design.

Clarion tech.

“They’re already here,” Mara said, her voice dropping low. “They’ve been threading this place for days. That wasn’t a ceiling fall. It was a shaped charge. They’re not just exploring. They’re excavating. And we’re in their dig site.”

As if summoned by the words, a new light speared into the cavern from a high ledge to the east. Not the soft glow of fungi. The harsh, white beam of an LED lantern. Then another. Voices, filtered and tinny through comms units, echoed down.

“—structural collapse in Sector Gamma. Proceed to scan for organic remains.”

“Copy. Sweeping.”

Mara met my eyes. The fury was still there, but it was now fused with a cold, clear understanding. We were both organic remains waiting to be scanned. And confirmed.

“Come on,” she hissed, grabbing my sleeve. “Unless your witnessing includes a detailed post-mortem.”

She moved with a feral, silent grace, ducking between the stalagmites, away from the lights. I followed. Our partnership, forged in the impact of my tackle, was now cemented in the simple, animal need to avoid the lights. It was not alliance. It was a temporary convergence of two fugitive vectors.

She led us to the far wall of the cavern, to a fissure I would have missed entirely—a vertical crack just wide enough to turn shoulders sideways. She slid in without hesitation. I followed, the stone squeezing my chest, my pack scraping painfully. The voices grew louder behind us, beams sweeping the floor where we’d just been.

We emerged into a smaller tunnel, this one clearly not natural. The walls were tool-marked, ancient pickaxes having clawed this path out of the rock centuries, maybe millennia, ago. The air smelled of old metal and damp.

We didn’t stop until the sounds of pursuit faded into the general drip and sigh of the cave. We crouched in the dark, listening to our own ragged breathing.

“Why are you here, Mara?” I asked quietly.

She was examining the crack on her temple with a small mirror. “Same as you. I seek.”

“For whom?”

“For myself.” She put the mirror away. “My great-grandmother was part of the last expedition to map these coastal caves. 1923. She came back with… stories. And a sickness in her lungs that nothing could cure. She said the cave breathed its own time, and it didn’t agree with human clocks.” Mara’s voice was flat, reciting a family epitaph. “The Clarion bought her journals at auction. I’m here to see what they thought was worth purchasing. And to see it before they turn it into a drilling schematic or a shareholder report.”

I thought of Elara’s journal in my pack. The love she carried for a world that was indifferent. The mortality it had exacted. “They’re not the only ones with resources.”

“The Company?” She spat the word. “You’re the other side of the same coin. You aestheticize the mystery. You make it a consumable. The Clarion wants to dissect it. You want to frame it. Both of you kill it.”

I had no rebuttal. In the darkness, her words felt true. “The voice from the pool,” I said, changing the subject. “Did you hear it?”

She went very still. “You reached the Throat Chamber?”

“It sealed me in.”

A flicker of something like respect crossed her face, gone as quickly as it came. “I used a different ingress. An old blowhole. I bypassed the chamber. I’ve read the accounts. The ‘voice’ is a psychosomatic effect. Infrasound from the sea interacting with the chamber’s resonance. It primes the brain for auditory hallucination.”

Her explanation was clean, clinical. It should have been comforting. It wasn’t. It felt like describing a hurricane as a difference in barometric pressure.

“It knew I carried the love,” I said, more to myself than to her.

Mara was silent for a long moment. Then she said, “The old accounts… they don’t call it a voice. They call it the Echo of the First Drowning. It doesn’t speak. It reflects. It shows you what you bring.” She looked at me, her dark eyes unreadable. “What love do you carry, Alex?”

Before I could answer—before I even knew what the answer was—a new sound echoed down the mining tunnel. Not voices. Not rock.

The rhythmic, synchronized tread of booted feet. At least two pairs. Coming our way.

Mara pressed herself against the wall. I killed my flashlight. We were trapped in a tube of stone with only one direction to go: forward, towards the sound, or back towards the cavern and the other searchers.

She pointed ahead, then held up two fingers. Two of them. Then she pointed to her ear, and made a slicing motion across her throat. Silent take-down.

I shook my head. I was a witness, not an operative. I carried a multi-tool, not a knife.

She rolled her eyes, a gesture of profound exasperation visible even in the near-dark. Reaching into her pack, she pulled out a small, flat canister. She mouthed a word: Thermite.

She pointed to the tunnel ceiling above where the footsteps would pass. It was laced with thick, ancient timber supports, black with age. She mimed placing the canister, activating it, and then retreating.

It was a diversion. A violent, potentially catastrophic one.

The footsteps grew closer. A beam of light swept the tunnel floor ahead.

I nodded.

We moved like shadows forward. The timber was directly over the path. Mara, lighter and quicker, climbed the rough wall using the timber ends as holds, placed the small device in a crevice where two beams met, and slid back down. We retreated twenty yards back around a slight bend.
